[01:18 - 01:20] One was the perpetuity approach,

### 3. DCF-Modeling-Book-Value-vs-Market-Value

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_DCF/DCF-Modeling-Book-Value-vs-Market-Value/transcript/DCF-Modeling-Book-Value-vs-Market-Value.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_DCF\DCF-Modeling-Book-Value-vs-Market-Value\transcript\DCF-Modeling-Book-Value-vs-Market-Value.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:05] Now let's talk about another way to think about value and that's book value versus market value.
[00:05 - 00:13] So the question now is does our hot dog stand really have equity of 450 in an enterprise value of 900 like we just calculated?
[00:13 - 00:22] Most businesses of course are worth far more than their book value and the balance sheet that we have is just measuring how much money I originally put into the business.
[00:22 - 00:26] That doesn't necessarily equate to what the business is currently worth.
[00:26 - 00:42] The hot dog stand might be worth a lot because word has gotten up and as a result I'm going to generate a whole bunch of cash flows that the present value which is, you know, would suggest a market value significantly higher than the original book value that the enterprise value that I just calculated measures.
[00:42 - 00:47] So now the question becomes and I've already alluded to one of these tools which is discounted future cash flows.
[00:47 - 00:51] The question is what tools do we have to determine the value of the business?
[00:51 - 01:07] So moving away from our hot dog example for a publicly traded company, the equity market, the stock market value is readily observable just by looking at the company's share price and multiplying it by the shares that are outstanding in the market that's called market cap.
[01:07 - 01:13] And that gives us the total market derived equity market derived value of the business.
[01:13 - 01:18] Let's take a look at Google and just understand the basic formulation of market cap.
[01:18 - 01:28] So if Google has an equity value on its financial statements, if you open up its balance sheet, you'll see that it has a book value of equity of $75 billion.
[01:28 - 01:31] That's the information on its latest 10Q.
[01:31 - 01:33] But that's not the market value of Google's equity.
[01:33 - 01:40] If you look at Google's share price, it trades at the market as of the time of printing at around $875 per share.
[01:40 - 01:50] With $337 million shares outstanding, that implies a rough equity market cap of $290 billion, significantly higher than the $75 billion you see on its books.
[01:50 - 02:01] Now, if you want to go back up to enterprise value, if that gives you equity value, since Google has about $50 billion in cash, $5 billion in debt per the company's latest 10Q,
[02:01 - 02:08] well, you can back into an enterprise value using the equation that we just introduced as something like $245 billion.
[02:08 - 02:14] Since they have a lot of cash, the enterprise value, the value of the core operations is actually lower than the value of their equity.
[02:14 - 02:20] And that's because they have a whole bunch of cash, which enterprise value doesn't capture, but equity value, of course, does.
[02:20 - 02:25] Well, we have yet to address is exactly how that share price is arrived at.
[02:25 - 02:27] Where does that come from?
[02:27 - 02:33] And what are the methodologies that we have in place to really arrive at a fair value or at a correct value for a company?
[02:33 - 02:43] Now that we know that there's a difference between book values and the actual market value, or the actual fair value, we're going to start talking about the differences between those concepts.
[02:43 - 02:50] In order to do that, in our next video, we start introducing some of the key valuation methodologies that we have to start going through that process.

### 4. DCF-Modeling-Calculating-LTM-Data

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_DCF/DCF-Modeling-Calculating-LTM-Data/transcript/DCF-Modeling-Calculating-LTM-Data.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_DCF\DCF-Modeling-Calculating-LTM-Data\transcript\DCF-Modeling-Calculating-LTM-Data.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:03] What I want to show is, unlike the simple DCF,
[00:03 - 00:05] where I didn't do an LTM, a last 12 months,
[00:05 - 00:08] I sort of had to look at year one multiples and said,
[00:08 - 00:10] oh, okay, well, based on my DCF,
[00:10 - 00:13] I think that the multiples can be 17.4 times,
[00:13 - 00:16] but here's a range based on different assumptions.
[00:16 - 00:17] Well, now let's look at LTM multiples.
[00:17 - 00:20] This is, you don't need an integrated model for this,
[00:20 - 00:22] but I just wanted to give you another look.
[00:22 - 00:25] I want to be able to look at LTM EBITDA multiples.
[00:25 - 00:29] So in order to do that, I have to calculate
[00:29 - 00:31] what is the enterprise value,
[00:31 - 00:33] which I have divided by what?
[00:33 - 00:35] What's my LTM EBITDA?
[00:35 - 00:39] And so, the easiest way to do this
[00:39 - 00:42] is to look at the, so we create a column.
[00:42 - 00:43] It says last 12 months,
[00:43 - 00:47] the LTM is starting February 28th, 2019.
[00:47 - 00:51] So the next thing I need is LTM and I look and I say,
[00:51 - 00:55] okay, well, 58.9% of this year is yet to happen,
[00:55 - 00:58] so I need to take one minus that.
[00:58 - 01:01] That's the portion of the current year that's already happened.
[01:01 - 01:04] So take the revenue, the presumably have already happened.
[01:04 - 01:10] Plus the portion of the last actual year,
[01:10 - 01:11] that gets you the last 12 months.
[01:11 - 01:15] And I'm fully aware that I'm ignoring seasonality.
[01:15 - 01:18] I'm ignoring quarterly disclosures.
[01:18 - 01:21] This is, in reality, a back of the envelope way to do this.
[01:21 - 01:23] If you have quarterly data by all means use it,
[01:23 - 01:27] but if you don't, just use this LTM formulation,
[01:27 - 01:28] where you just take the fraction of the year,
[01:28 - 01:31] of the first forecast year, that's already happened,
[01:31 - 01:34] and added to the fraction of the last year
[01:34 - 01:36] that represents the remaining period.

### 7. DCF-Modeling-Cash-Flow-vs-Relative-Valuation

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_DCF/DCF-Modeling-Cash-Flow-vs-Relative-Valuation/transcript/DCF-Modeling-Cash-Flow-vs-Relative-Valuation.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_DCF\DCF-Modeling-Cash-Flow-vs-Relative-Valuation\transcript\DCF-Modeling-Cash-Flow-vs-Relative-Valuation.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:03] So let's get back to the question. How do investors determine the right price?
[00:03 - 00:12] There are a couple of frameworks to do that, and specifically there are two broad frameworks that are most common in practice to value or to determine the right price.
[00:12 - 00:17] The first framework is the DCF, and that's the topic for our course today.
[00:17 - 00:27] It's called also an intrinsic analysis, and it's derived from the fundamental analysis of a company's cash flow generation potential.
[00:27 - 00:34] It basically looks at the value of a business as the present value of all the future cash flows that business is expected to generate.
[00:34 - 00:46] The other approach, called relative valuation or comparable company analysis, which is the topic of a subsequent course, and that's derived by comparing a company to its comparable peers.
[00:46 - 00:58] In fact, when you're looking at a house, the most common way to value a house is by looking at its peers, by looking at similar houses down the street because there are a lot of very similar comparable, so you can pick from.
[00:58 - 01:04] You can think about both of those approaches as really the two broad frameworks that we can pick from when value in companies.
[01:04 - 01:15] But just to understand that while the DCF and comps seem quite different, of course, one just looks at other companies to figure out the value, and the other one looks at the future cash flow generation potential.
[01:15 - 01:19] That seems like those seem like two very distinct ways of figuring out a value.
[01:19 - 01:22] But in fact, they're quite related.
[01:22 - 01:30] So much so that in theory, a DCF should exactly equal or yield the same value as comps analysis should.
[01:30 - 01:36] Of course, that equality rarely holds precisely for reasons that we will get into later in the advanced topic section.
[01:36 - 01:41] But theoretically, they are fundamentally intertwined.
[01:41 - 01:45] In the next video, we turn to the meat of the course, the DCF approach.

---

[01:02 - 01:06] But if they had itemize it on the balance sheet, what you'd actually see listed is 966.

[01:14 - 01:19] So the balance sheet actually shows you principal balances after deducting transaction costs.
[01:19 - 01:23] Whereas in the notes, you actually see the aggregate principal amount in for our purposes.

[01:33 - 01:36] The truth of the matter, as you see here, it's not really a material difference usually.

[03:10 - 03:13] And the value as debt is 978.

### 13. DCF-Modeling-DCF-Implementation

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:06] Before starting the modeling exercise, let's review the key takeaways so far.
[00:06 - 00:11] We talked about the prevalent form of the DCF being a two-stage DCF model.
[00:11 - 00:14] There are, of course, other types of DCF models.
[00:14 - 00:17] There are three-stage DCF models.
[00:17 - 00:20] Models called, there's a model called the high-low DCF model.
[00:20 - 00:24] They're all possible and they all work just fine, but they're less used in practice.
[00:24 - 00:29] So we're going to focus and build the model that's actually used widely in practice and that's a two-stage model.
[00:29 - 00:34] The first-stage typically in practice is where you forecast the explicit, unlevver-free cash flows.
[00:34 - 00:37] The typical forecast period is five to ten years.
[00:37 - 00:41] Those of you who have just finished our financial statement modeling course
[00:41 - 00:45] recall that we did forecast Apple for five years.
[00:45 - 00:50] So we're going to continue that case study or we're going to build our DCF using that Apple case study.
[00:50 - 00:54] Stage two is calculating the terminal value.
[00:54 - 00:57] In other words, saying, well, after five years worth of forecast,
[00:57 - 01:00] we assume that Apple will continue to generate cash flows.
[01:00 - 01:05] But we're not going to explicitly build forecast off a three-state in the model at this point.
[01:05 - 01:10] Instead, we're just going to make some generalizing assumptions and calculate what's called the terminal value.
[01:10 - 01:14] We add the present values as of today of both stages together.
[01:14 - 01:21] Of course, by discounting using the WAC, that gives us the enterprise value or the value of the operations.
[01:21 - 01:26] And now, just make sure that we understand this completely and intuitively,
[01:26 - 01:29] once we get this value of operations,
[01:29 - 01:35] we need to, of course, to get to equity value because ultimately our goal is to figure out what is the fair value per share.
[01:35 - 01:39] And fair value, fair value per share so you can compare it to the stock price
[01:39 - 01:41] and figure out whether this company is overvalued or undervalued,
[01:41 - 01:46] what we have to get to equity value and then have to divide it by shares outstanding to get to equity value per share.
[01:46 - 01:49] Well, how do we go from enterprise value to equity value?
[01:49 - 01:52] Well, we know that we subtract by net debt.
[01:52 - 01:57] But let's conceptually understand what the components of net debt are and why it makes and how everything works.
[01:57 - 02:04] So if we want to figure out what is the total really value of the operations plus any non operating assets,
[02:04 - 02:08] what we take enterprise value plus any non operating assets, namely cash.
[02:08 - 02:13] And we say this together represents the value of the operations.
[02:13 - 02:18] This whole thing, the enterprise value plus the value of non operating assets.
[02:18 - 02:21] This represents the enterprise value plus the non operating assets.
[02:21 - 02:25] But of course, we have some non equity financial claims against the business.
[02:25 - 02:30] So before the equity owners can sort of figure out what value belongs to them,
[02:30 - 02:35] we have to subtract out from this piece all the non equity claims against the business.
[02:35 - 02:38] And this is the resulting equity value.
[02:38 - 02:42] And if you divide this by shares outstanding, you get equity value per share.
[02:42 - 02:44] So this is the process that we're going to go through.
[02:44 - 02:50] In terms of the stages, stage one is unlevered free cash flows.
[02:50 - 02:57] And in terms of implementing, we are going to link these cash flows directly off of the financial statement model that we built for Apple.
[02:57 - 03:00] This is the optimal way to forecast.
[03:00 - 03:04] Of course, you can also do back of the envelope DCF models where you don't need a three statement model.
[03:04 - 03:11] But of course, the more rigorous approach is to build free cash flows off of a full three statement model.
[03:11 - 03:16] Stage two represents the terminal value, the terminal value.
[03:16 - 03:18] They're actually two ways to do this.
[03:18 - 03:25] The first way is that what we've been talking about already and that's using the growth and perpetuity formula approach where we say,
[03:25 - 03:29] okay, well, what is Apple's free cash flow in the last forecast year?
[03:29 - 03:32] Let's grow that by one plus some G, which we haven't talked about.
[03:32 - 03:33] How do we arrive at?
[03:33 - 03:34] What is it?
[03:34 - 03:37] What is it potentially terminal growth rate for a company?
[03:37 - 03:39] We'll talk about that later on.
[03:39 - 03:42] Then Apple's terminal value.
[03:42 - 03:48] In other words, it's value as of the end of the forecast, the stage one forecast period can be derived this way.
[03:48 - 03:57] And then we need to discount that all the way to the present because the growth and perpetuity formula is going to get us to the very end of stage one.
[03:57 - 04:03] So if we stage two represents all cash flows beyond stage one, this discounts us to the end of stage one.
[04:03 - 04:07] And then we got to have to discount it a second time to get to the beginning of stage one.
[04:07 - 04:11] There is another approach to do this whole thing called the exit multiple approach.
[04:11 - 04:14] We're going to talk about that in just a little bit.
[04:14 - 04:17] Last but not least, the weighted average cost of capital.
[04:17 - 04:22] The formula I've laid out here, we're going to spend a lot of time just conceptually breaking this formula down.
[04:22 - 04:29] For now, the only thing that you need to understand is that we are taking the cost of debt.
[04:29 - 04:39] We are adjusting it by the fraction of the capital structure that's funded with debt.
[04:39 - 04:41] We're multiplying those two together.
[04:41 - 04:44] This tax adjustment represents the fact that interest is tax deductible.
[04:44 - 04:46] So we sort of alluded to this in just a moment.
[04:46 - 04:53] And then we're adding this debt weight to the equity weight.
[04:53 - 05:00] The cost of equity is weighted at the portion of the capital structure that's funded via equity.
[05:00 - 05:08] In terms of implementing and calculating all of this, we're going to discuss that truly for now just understand the basic formula structure here.
[05:08 - 05:18] Understand that because the DCF is so sensitive to assumptions, whether there be the free cash flow assumptions, the terminal value assumptions, the discount rate assumptions,
[05:18 - 05:25] you're always going to present the DCF, not as just one number, but as rather an output range.
[05:25 - 05:29] You're going to present a range of potential DCF valuations.
[05:29 - 05:37] The key assumptions to sensitize are always going to be the discount rate, revenue operating margin assumptions, as well as terminal value assumptions.

### 16. DCF-Modeling-DCF-Overview

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_DCF/DCF-Modeling-DCF-Overview/transcript/DCF-Modeling-DCF-Overview.transcript.txt`

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:02] So, let's talk about the DCF.
[00:02 - 00:08] Specifically, the DCF valuation values a business as the sum of all the cash flows that
[00:08 - 00:13] it will generate discounted to the present value at a rate that reflects the riskiness
[00:13 - 00:16] of the cash flows associated with the business.
[00:16 - 00:20] And when we define cash flows, we're going to get very granular about how we define
[00:20 - 00:21] cash flows.
[00:21 - 00:23] This can be a topic in and of itself.
[00:23 - 00:27] But broadly speaking, let's just understand that we are defining the cash flows that
[00:27 - 00:32] the business can annually generate and provide to its providers of capital.
[00:32 - 00:39] As the cash flows that emerge from operations, less any required reinvestments and when
[00:39 - 00:44] I say required cash reinvestments, I mean, cash that needs to be reinvested into the business
[00:44 - 00:48] in order to sustain the sort of growth in cash flows that we are forecasting in the first
[00:48 - 00:49] place.
[00:49 - 00:55] And so, a simple way to think about that is if a business is generating a certain operating,
[00:55 - 01:00] amount of operating cash flows, it will need to continually reinvest some of those
[01:00 - 01:01] cash flows.
[01:01 - 01:05] At least a portion of those cash flows back into the business via capital expenditures
[01:05 - 01:10] and other types of purchases in order to generate the growth that you are forecasting
[01:10 - 01:13] in the first place.
[01:13 - 01:19] The discount rate that we are going to use in order to arrive at the present value of
[01:19 - 01:24] a business should represent the required rate of return that the investor seek on
[01:25 - 01:30] the investment of the required rate of return that an investor would expect to generate
[01:30 - 01:36] by putting their money at risk in the first place.
[01:36 - 01:43] And so, the more the risk the investor believes they are taking on by investing in your
[01:43 - 01:46] business, the more, and specifically when we're talking about risk, we're talking about
[01:46 - 01:51] riskiness of the cash flows that are being forecast, the higher the risk of those cash
[01:51 - 01:57] flows, the higher the expected return, the required rate of return that the investor
[01:57 - 01:59] will seek.
[01:59 - 02:06] And so, the discount rate in and of itself is a topic that can consume a full semester's
[02:06 - 02:08] worth of corporate finance.
[02:08 - 02:13] So we are going to talk about it just in the context at first of sort of the mathematical
[02:13 - 02:16] requirement of having a discount rate and then we're going to really get into what this
[02:16 - 02:18] discount rate should truly represent.
[02:18 - 02:26] It's actually a fairly controversial part of the DCF analysis.
[02:26 - 02:30] Broadly speaking, we've got a formula here down below which you can see which identifies
[02:30 - 02:37] really what is the heart of intrinsic analysis which says the value of a business today is
[02:37 - 02:43] a function of the sum of all future cash flows discounted to the present at some appropriate

[03:06 - 03:07] valuing?
[03:07 - 03:12] Similarly, if you were to increase the discount rate of the business, in other words,
[03:12 - 03:19] if you're going to, if you believe that the cash flows, the four, those cash flow forecasts,
[03:19 - 03:21] there's more risk to those cash flows.

[03:23 - 03:24] Give that a shot.
[03:24 - 03:27] We'll go through that together as a group.
[03:27 - 03:28] Well that was fairly straightforward.
[03:28 - 03:33] I suspect most of you came up with the right answer, then obviously all that SQL,
[03:33 - 03:41] the higher the forecasted cash flows, the higher the effect on value.
[03:41 - 03:48] And conversely, the higher perceived risk for the set cash flows, the higher the discount
[03:48 - 03:53] rate will be, and as a result, the lower the value will be.
[03:53 - 03:56] So now let's get into some of the DCF mechanics.

[08:34 - 08:41] year and faced a forever discount rate of 10%, and a forever growth rate of 5%.

### 18. DCF-Modeling-DCF-vs-Comps-in-the-Real-World-Part-2

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_DCF/DCF-Modeling-DCF-vs-Comps-in-the-Real-World-Part-2/transcript/DCF-Modeling-DCF-vs-Comps-in-the-Real-World-Part-2.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_DCF\DCF-Modeling-DCF-vs-Comps-in-the-Real-World-Part-2\transcript\DCF-Modeling-DCF-vs-Comps-in-the-Real-World-Part-2.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:05] Now let's talk about advantages and disadvantages of the DCF in contrast to comps.
[00:05 - 00:10] So the DCF is actually widely used both in practice and is respected academically.
[00:10 - 00:16] The comps are not as respected academically and that's really because the idea is no two companies are exactly alike
[00:16 - 00:20] and theoretically speaking the value of a company should just be a function of its future cash flows.
[00:20 - 00:27] And so the DCF advantage is that academically it is something that is actually respected in addition to it being used in practice.
[00:27 - 00:31] It is theoretically the most sound method evaluation.
[00:31 - 00:36] It also from a practical standpoint enables you to value individual pieces of a business.
[00:36 - 00:42] As long as you can make assumptions about what are the cash flows at a certain part of the business can generate,
[00:42 - 00:44] well then you can value that business individually.
[00:44 - 00:48] You can't really do that using comps as easily.
[00:48 - 00:54] And perhaps the last but not least, perhaps the most important part of why the DCF is useful
[00:54 - 00:58] is that it is not influenced by current market pricing.
[00:58 - 01:08] And so if you are concerned that the market is wholesale mispricing stocks, mispricing assets and businesses.
[01:08 - 01:13] Whether it's a bull market or a bear market and you want a sanity check against the market.
[01:13 - 01:16] Well there's no better tool than the DCF.
[01:16 - 01:18] It is independent of the market.
[01:18 - 01:23] It basically looks at hey what do I realistically think can happen from a cash flow generation perspective
[01:23 - 01:26] for this company and you arrive at a value that way.
[01:26 - 01:32] And that becomes a very powerful tool to counteract any potential mispricing in the market.
[01:32 - 01:37] The counter argument of course is if you believe in efficient markets, the question would be,
[01:37 - 01:41] well isn't the market the best way to value the business?
[01:41 - 01:50] Doesn't the market implicitly already capture all of the market's cash flow expectations within the pricing of the equity?
[01:50 - 01:59] And so there is this tension between the efficient market, folks that say the stock market is a efficiently priced market.
[01:59 - 02:05] And all the information about future cash flows is implicitly already baked into the equity price.
[02:05 - 02:09] And so you don't need to worry about the DCF the market price is the right price.
[02:09 - 02:10] That's the tension.
[02:10 - 02:15] The folks that argue that versus the folks that say, well the market can be, can misprice.
[02:15 - 02:19] And we should use the DCF to sanity check against that.
[02:19 - 02:27] The problem with the DCF and the reason why we often use the comps in concert with the DCF is that there's no real consensus on implementation.
[02:27 - 02:37] All the assumptions that go into the DCF is we're going to see shortly, not least of which is for example estimating the discount rate and specifically estimating the cost of capital.
[02:37 - 02:41] The methodology surrounding that is incredibly controversial.
[02:41 - 02:48] In addition, in order to really build a good DCF analysis, you need pretty extensive company financials.
[02:48 - 02:57] The DCF is also extremely sensitive to changes in operating assumptions, terminal value assumptions, and of course the cost of capital assumptions.
[02:57 - 03:07] The DCF perhaps more than any other model is one of those models where if you put garbage in, you'll get garbage out.
[03:07 - 03:13] You can get your DCF to say whatever you want it to say with a very small change in assumptions.

---

### 20. DCF-Modeling-Enterprise-Value-vs-Equity-Value

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_DCF/DCF-Modeling-Enterprise-Value-vs-Equity-Value/transcript/DCF-Modeling-Enterprise-Value-vs-Equity-Value.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_DCF\DCF-Modeling-Enterprise-Value-vs-Equity-Value\transcript\DCF-Modeling-Enterprise-Value-vs-Equity-Value.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:05] At this point, we can actually introduce enterprise value versus equity value.
[00:05 - 00:09] On the surface, and I suspect some of you are already familiar with the basic equations
[00:09 - 00:11] to get from equity value and enterprise value and vice versa.
[00:11 - 00:14] But many aren't exactly clear.
[00:14 - 00:18] Even certain investment banking professionals, finance professionals have been using the
[00:18 - 00:20] concepts for a long time.
[00:20 - 00:24] Some of them are not even perfectly clear on what these concepts are and how exactly they're
[00:24 - 00:27] really relate to one another.
[00:27 - 00:30] The definition of enterprise value, most people will sort of think about enterprise
[00:30 - 00:36] value and they say, oh, enterprise value equals equity value plus net debt.
[00:36 - 00:37] That's one way to look at enterprise value.
[00:37 - 00:41] That's a way to define enterprise value as how it's financed.
[00:41 - 00:45] That's finance with equity and net debt.
[00:45 - 00:47] But let's look at enterprise value more directly.
[00:47 - 00:49] What does it actually represent?
[00:49 - 00:54] Well, enterprise value actually represents the operating assets, net of any operating liabilities.
[00:54 - 00:59] When we think about operating assets, we usually mean all assets except for cash.
[00:59 - 01:04] When we think about operating liabilities, we're generally referring to all liabilities except
[01:04 - 01:06] for debt and debt-like liabilities.
[01:06 - 01:13] We think of things like non-controlling or minority interests, preferred stock.
[01:13 - 01:17] One very important point here, rather than looking at cash as some part of operations, it's
[01:17 - 01:20] simply considered as just an offset to debt.
[01:20 - 01:24] So if you have a whole bunch of debt and some cash, it's treated as if you don't have
[01:24 - 01:31] any cash and you just have a net debt figure for the purposes of definitions here.
[01:31 - 01:33] So let's make sure that we understand this.
[01:33 - 01:35] So here's a simple exercise.
[01:35 - 01:41] Given the balance sheet that we have, try to calculate what is the enterprise value, what
[01:41 - 01:44] is the net debt, and of course the resulting equity value.
[01:44 - 01:49] Give this a shot yourself when you're ready, hit unpause, and we'll go through it together
[01:49 - 01:52] as a group.
[01:52 - 01:57] So the enterprise value, for defining the enterprise value is the operating assets, net
[01:57 - 02:02] of operating liabilities, we'll here are our operating assets, remember we're not including
[02:02 - 02:09] cash as an operating assets, we treat that as just an offset to debt.
[02:09 - 02:12] Operating liabilities is just the AP.
[02:12 - 02:21] So 920 minus 20 gets us to an enterprise value of 900.
[02:21 - 02:30] net debt represents the total debt that we have outstanding net of any cash for 50.
[02:30 - 02:35] Not of course gets us to the equity value which we already knew because the equity value
[02:35 - 02:40] is already something that's listed on the balance sheet is 450.
[02:40 - 02:48] So understand that this is just a reformulation of the traditional balance sheet to identify
[02:48 - 02:52] the portion of the assets, net of the portion of liabilities that represents the core
[02:52 - 02:56] operations of the business, and that's the enterprise value.
[02:56 - 03:02] And if you subtract from the core operating value of the business net debt, it's non-operating
[03:02 - 03:08] assets and non-operating liabilities, well you'll get to the equity value.
[03:08 - 03:12] And that's just another way of looking at the balance sheet, the traditional balance sheet
[03:12 - 03:16] equations says, let me see all the assets that I have, whether they're operating or
[03:16 - 03:20] non-operating, let me subtract all the liabilities that I have, whether they're operating
[03:20 - 03:22] or non-operating, and that'll give me that 450.
[03:22 - 03:27] This is just a way of looking at it where you're bifurcating operations versus non-operating
[03:27 - 03:30] assets and liabilities.
[03:30 - 03:31] It's a very important distinction.
[03:31 - 03:39] It's because in finance, this relationship is actually used in valuation much more frequently
[03:39 - 03:44] than this relationship, so just make sure you understand the distinction.
[03:45 - 03:47] It's going to come up the time and again.
[03:47 - 03:48] And so I'll bring the point home.
[03:48 - 03:54] Enterprise value minus net debt equals equity value, and that is just a slight
[03:54 - 03:58] reformulation of the basic accounting equation, whereas when you're looking at
[03:58 - 04:03] enterprise value relative to equity value, you're basically trying to separate
[04:03 - 04:08] value of operations versus non-operating assets and liabilities.
[04:08 - 04:13] The one thing that I will stress here is a frequent source of confusion.
[04:13 - 04:17] Enterprise value does not equal the value of the entire business.
[04:17 - 04:22] It only measures the value of the core operations, whereas equity value measures the
[04:22 - 04:28] portion of the entire business that belongs to the equity owners.
[04:28 - 04:33] So if we go back to the slide, and I lost the writing, so let me just put it back here.
[04:33 - 04:43] If we go back to the slide and recognize that enterprise value is the value of core operations,
[04:43 - 04:48] recognize that this 900 is not the total value of the business, as is sometimes enterprise
[04:48 - 04:51] values confused with the total value of the business.
[04:51 - 04:54] It only represents the core value of the business.
[04:54 - 04:58] Remember, the company has a whole bunch of cash even in our simple balance sheet that isn't
[04:58 - 05:01] being picked up in the enterprise value.
[05:01 - 05:07] The equity value, on the other hand, does capture all the cash that a company has, but
[05:07 - 05:16] is also after taking out all the non-equity financial claims on the business.
[05:16 - 05:18] So just make sure that you understand the distinction.
[05:18 - 05:24] With enterprise value, that represents the value of the operations of the business, meaning
[05:24 - 05:27] core operating assets, which exclude cash.
[05:27 - 05:32] That's the big asset that's considered not an operating asset, but rather a financial
[05:32 - 05:37] type asset, a non operating asset, net of operating liabilities, think of things like
[05:37 - 05:39] accounts payable, working capital liabilities.
[05:39 - 05:42] That represents the core operating value of the business.
[05:42 - 05:46] And then when we look at equity value, we actually subtract net debt.
[05:46 - 05:51] In other words, we add back that non operating cash and subtract all the gross debt to
[05:51 - 05:56] represent equity value, which really in a colloquial way just represents the value that
[05:56 - 06:01] belongs in the pockets of the equity owners, the common equity owners.
[06:01 - 06:06] Now in the next video, we are going to sort of expand on this discussion a little bit,
[06:06 - 06:10] keeping in mind that up till now with this hot dog stand, we've really been exclusively
[06:10 - 06:12] looking at book values.
[06:12 - 06:15] And now we're going to start trying to understand, well, this enterprise value, how do
[06:15 - 06:20] we think about this enterprise value in the context of a true value, a more market-based
[06:20 - 06:21] value?
[06:21 - 06:25] And similarly, how do we think of equity value from a more market-based perspective,
[06:25 - 06:29] from a more intrinsic-based perspective, keeping in mind, of course, the hot dog stand
[06:29 - 06:33] and we just looked at, we've been arriving in a value based purely on how much we've
[06:33 - 06:35] spent, how much we've put into the business.
[06:35 - 06:40] As we know, as I suspect many of you know, the idea of value in the business is not so much
[06:40 - 06:42] a function of how much money you originally put into it.
[06:42 - 06:46] But what types of expectations you can put into this business?
[06:46 - 06:50] What types of cash flows can this business be expected to generate and how do we formulate
[06:50 - 06:54] that into an actual valuation from the business, both from the perspective of an enterprise
[06:54 - 06:55] value but also of equity value?

### 22. DCF-Modeling-Exit-Multiple-Concepts

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_DCF/DCF-Modeling-Exit-Multiple-Concepts/transcript/DCF-Modeling-Exit-Multiple-Concepts.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_DCF\DCF-Modeling-Exit-Multiple-Concepts\transcript\DCF-Modeling-Exit-Multiple-Concepts.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:10] So we've already talked about, and we just finished modeling the terminal value calculation where you're using a growth in perpetuity equation, and you're assigning a long-term growth rate.
[00:10 - 00:27] So we talked about that, and we talked about some of the limitations. In fact, we finished our model, the terminal value calculation, and our model talking about some of the limitations of the terminal value calculation, specifically the sort of general nature of calculating a long-term growth rate that regresses the 3 to 5% for just about every company.
[00:28 - 00:37] And so instead of using the growth in perpetuity equation, what ends up happening is the practitioners end up often using something called the exomultable method to calculate terminal value.
[00:37 - 00:50] And the exomultable method basically arrives at a terminal value by multiplying the company's last 12 months, EBITDA, by an LTM EBITDA multiple.
[00:50 - 00:59] And the multiple is usually derived from a trading comps or transaction comps analysis for that company, ultimately depending on the purpose of the DCF.
[00:59 - 01:17] So for example, if you're building a DCF for a standalone valuation, you're going to use a trading comps derived multiple because that reflects a standalone valuation, whereas if you are trying to understand the value of a company in the context of a potential acquisition, where someone might be acquiring a company, the company that you're looking at,
[01:17 - 01:25] will certainly that would incorporate some premiums and therefore a transaction comps analysis would be the appropriate analysis to derive the appropriate multiple.
[01:25 - 01:38] Now, the advantage in why it's ultimately used even more frequently than the growth in perpetuity approach is because it requires fewer explicit assumptions about future cash flows growth and it's more realistic.
[01:38 - 01:55] In other words, you don't need to explicitly say, you know, I think the long term growth rate is going to be 4%, which as we understand and as we just looked at in our model is sort of difficult to sort of justify or to work through every company ultimately is going to have that long term growth rate of 4%.
[01:55 - 02:11] The nice thing about using the exit multiple is you can look at the actual company's current multiple, the multiple that it currently fetches and say, hey, we think that five years from now at the end of stage one, this is the multiple that the company is going to have is going to benefit from.
[02:11 - 02:15] So it reflects a more realistic or market driven multiple.
[02:15 - 02:30] Now, the major downside of this and some of you may be already thinking about this is that if the entire benefit of the DCF analysis is it provides some sort of context, some sort of intrinsic counterweight to market valuations.
[02:30 - 02:54] Well, what have you done by incorporating a fundamentally market driven multiple into the analysis? You have infected the analysis with a market derived valuation that for most companies, the terminal value represents a significant part of the total value that's derived in the DCF and all of the sudden now it incorporates a market based valuation to arrive at that value.
[02:54 - 03:03] The whole benefit remember of the DCF is that it's saying, hey, the market may be wrong, the market may be overvaluing this business, the market may be undervaluing this business.
[03:03 - 03:20] This DCF is going to show us a market neutral perspective that's purely derived off of our cash flow expectations. Well, it's not when you're using the exit multiple approach and that's perhaps the most important thing to conceptually understand about the limitation of the exit multiple.
[03:20 - 03:30] It fundamentally creates a almost a mean reversion towards market values and that may or may not be desirable.
[03:30 - 03:39] In terms of the actual implementation, usually when we're talking about a terminal value exit multiple, we're almost always talking about EV to eBITDA multiple.
[03:39 - 03:45] It's by far the most common multiple, but you can theoretically use any enterprise value multiple when you're building an unlevered DCF.
[03:45 - 03:53] So, EV to revenue or EV to eBIT can all be used. You just have to be consistent across the board they're using last 12 months results.
[03:53 - 04:00] In a levered DCF, so if you're doing a levered DCF, obviously we're working through Apple's unlevered DCF.
[04:00 - 04:08] If you were to do the levered DCF approach, you would use a PE or a price to book multiple for your exit multiple.
[04:08 - 04:18] The final thing to keep in mind is just like with the perpetuity approach, the terminal value does need to be discounted back to the present using the weighted average cost of capital for the unlevered DCF analysis.

### 30. DCF-Modeling-Industry-Beta-Concepts

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_DCF/DCF-Modeling-Industry-Beta-Concepts/transcript/DCF-Modeling-Industry-Beta-Concepts.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_DCF\DCF-Modeling-Industry-Beta-Concepts\transcript\DCF-Modeling-Industry-Beta-Concepts.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:14] So now we talk about one of the problems with beta so we talked about some of the problems with the equity risk premium in terms of do we equity risk premium is fundamentally historical looking that may not necessarily title what's going to happen in the future.
[00:14 - 00:24] We talked about some of the issues surrounding calculating the cost of debt that the coupon rate isn't really the best the yield to maturity is probably better beta has even bigger problems.
[00:25 - 00:47] And so in terms of just finding beta understand that the way that beta is calculated is based is typically some sort of version of looking at the last 60 months looking at the last 60 months worth of S&P 500 returns and comparing those to the company that we're looking at.
[00:47 - 00:57] And those returns and sort of correlating those and basically building a regression across sort of the S&P 500 and our company over the last 60 months.
[00:57 - 01:16] Now different providers like Bloomberg and bear which sort of make a living and calculating beta try to sort of tweak that formula that regression to make it a little bit more relevant and they call it sort of a forward looking beta and they try to sort of mean regressive because they've noticed that all beta sort of ultimately regressed the one.
[01:16 - 01:25] But ultimately that is the primary methodology that the problem with that being the primary methodology is it is again fundamentally historical looking.
[01:25 - 01:38] Calculating betas from historical returns is an imprecise measurement especially if the beta that we derive from that if the regression that's derived from that historical relationship.
[01:38 - 01:41] Or yields a very high standard error.
[01:41 - 01:51] Many companies when you look at their betas historically over the last 60 months where you're trying to calculate the relationship between their returns to the returns of the S&P 500.
[01:51 - 01:57] You're going to get a correlative or you're going to get a the regression is going to provide you a beta.
[01:57 - 02:00] The question is how useful is that beta as a predictor.
[02:00 - 02:15] And if the standard error associated with that historical relationship is really low or really high I should say if the if the quality of the correlation is really poor the R squared is really low.
[02:15 - 02:29] Well then you have a problem because you're using the beta that you derived and understand that you can arrive at a at a sort of correlation between anything that doesn't necessarily have any sort of causation or any true fundamental relationship.
[02:29 - 02:37] I could I could create a beta for the last 60 months worth of raindrops compared to apples stock returns.
[02:37 - 02:46] It wouldn't necessarily be a good predictor would have a tremendously high standard air and if the useless is a predictor for what's going to happen to apple stock based on future raindrops.
[02:46 - 02:59] And so that's ultimately the problem with beta and then if you look historically the S&P 500 does not the historical sensitivity the S&P 500 isn't necessarily a good predictor because it has such a very high standard air.
[02:59 - 03:09] Part of the reason that it has such a very high standard air is because company specific issues or sort of dilute the quality of the correlation.
[03:09 - 03:11] And so what do we do about that?
[03:11 - 03:20] Understand that when you're dealing with a public company you can always look at it's you can always find its beta by going to Bloomberg or other services like notably beta.
[03:20 - 03:23] And they'll provide you this calculation.
[03:24 - 03:31] Now so with a public company you have a choice either you just take that that beta straight out as most practitioners do.
[03:31 - 03:42] Or you say hey you know if these betas have such high standard air is there a way for me to reduce the standard air part of this is there is some way that I can look at.
[03:42 - 03:49] Calculate beta in a way that filters out the noise of company specific events.
[03:49 - 03:52] Those are the issues that I have to wrestle with for public company.
[03:52 - 03:57] For private companies it's not even an option to look at the observable beta.
[03:57 - 04:03] So I have to think about some methodology I can't look at a beta for a private company it doesn't have an observable share price.
[04:03 - 04:10] So I have to think for private companies as well as for public companies where I think the beta is not a very good predictor.
[04:10 - 04:14] What do I do do I have a methodology in place and the answer is yes.
[04:14 - 04:20] We do have a methodology in place to try to improve the quality of beta.
[04:20 - 04:32] And that methodology is to not look at just your own company to calculate the beta which of course will be skewed with all these company specific issues that don't necessarily
[04:32 - 04:35] necessarily happen in the future.
[04:35 - 04:52] We can use the company's peers and it almost is through a diversification effect since they operate as long as you're taking true peers the sensitivity to the market should actually be fairly comparable and you reduce the noise of company specific issues.
[04:52 - 04:58] And so this is sort of this takes us to the industry beta methodology.
[04:58 - 05:08] And in fact this is a preferable methodology for calculating beta is opposed to simply just taking even for public companies the published beta.
[05:08 - 05:19] And so for private companies and for when public company betas have high standard errors one solution in fact the solution that's used in practice is to use what's called the industry beta.
[05:19 - 05:27] By looking at historical betas of a company's peer group with similar sensitivity to market fluctuations a private company's beta can be derived.
[05:27 - 05:31] And of course a public company with a high standard error can also be improved.
[05:31 - 05:43] The beta for that company can also be improved as the impact of uncorrelated company specific events the raises standard air will cancel each other out the more peers are added.
[05:43 - 05:54] The problem in terms of implementation is that the industry peers can have very different capital structures and capital structure has a very significant impact on beta.
[05:54 - 06:12] And so the challenge for us is to effectively take the capital structure that the company that we're looking at has and impose that and sort of take out the capital structure of the industry peers and try to understand what would beta look like for those peers with our company's capital structure.
[06:12 - 06:19] And so the way we do that is we undo the distorting of impact of different capital structures on beta.
[06:19 - 06:29] By de-levering those betas by a methodology called de-levering betas of the observed betas taking the leverage out of those betas.
[06:29 - 06:39] And of course the higher the leverage the higher the beta so we're really going to reduce the betas to what's called a de-levered beta which is a debt free beta.
[06:39 - 06:49] And then we are going to then impose our own or the our target companies leverage onto those de-levered betas in a process called re-levering beta.
[06:49 - 07:05] Understand that all else equal the reason why the higher the leverage a company has the higher the beta that's because the cash flows to equity holders are far more volatile when you have higher fixed interest payments.
[07:05 - 07:19] In other words, if the market is fluctuating and there's a lot of volatility in terms of how much cash flows the equity holders are going to expect to receive.
[07:19 - 07:29] Well, if you've got a big fixed interest payment sitting before you, well that enhances that juices up the volatility even further beyond just the general market volatility.
[07:29 - 07:36] And so again, the process is to de-lever the betas of comparable companies and then re-lever them at the target company's capital structure.
[07:36 - 07:38] Here is the process.
[07:38 - 07:45] To de-lever a company's beta, your industry peers beta, you take their observed beta.
[07:45 - 07:53] Again, these have to be public companies with observed betas that you would get from Bloomberg or Barrett just like you would have if you were directly taking the beta for a public company.
[07:53 - 08:03] And then you look at the peer groups, each of their individual specific tax rates and their capital structure.
[08:03 - 08:11] And you plug it into this equation to back the leverage out of the beta and that gives you a de-levered beta.
[08:11 - 08:25] Once you've calculated the de-levered beta of every one of your companies in your peer group, you take the average of that de-levered beta and then re-lever it.
[08:25 - 08:33] So you plug in, this is where the average peer group, so let's make sure we understand that.
[08:33 - 08:46] This is where the average peer-derived beta goes.
[08:46 - 09:00] And then you then, once again, de-lever at the target company's debt equity ratio and tax rate to get to the industry beta-levered at our target companies leverage.

[00:58 - 01:06] So we start with E44, E44 is the beta. We want to divide that by 1 plus 1 minus the tax rate.

[01:43 - 01:49] or just using the variables that they gave us. E45 and E46. It's a share price times that Ludus shares outstanding.

[02:01 - 02:11] and the same thing across the board. And obviously the one company that is highly levered in this equation, and that's Rite Aid, Rite Aid is significantly more levered.
[02:11 - 02:20] You can see their gross debt net of cash makes up almost half of, is almost the entire market cap.
[02:20 - 02:34] So they're almost at a 50-50 debt to equity ratio compared to the far lower rates of leverage for Walgreens and CVS, and Rite Aid is a company that's been historically troubled and has faced a high degree of leverage.
[02:34 - 02:41] And so you can see that they've got a far lower de-levered beta than they have their observed beta.
[02:41 - 02:54] And that's just a function to their leverage. And so had you taken Rite Aid without delivering it, you would have gotten an unreasonably high, unreasonably high beta, given that our company doesn't have such a high degree of leverage.

[03:05 - 03:23] And then the process of sort of re-levering it at the private company capital structure is a process of plugging the average de-levered beta, which we got at 1, back into the reverse of the equation, just multiplying this by this denominator here.
[03:23 - 03:30] So we bring that denominator back up to get it back to a levered beta, and we are good to go.
[03:30 - 03:32] So the answer is 1.26.

[03:28 - 03:37] But if Apple grows at 5% forever and has a far lower discount rate in actuality, well then it's worth $730.
[03:37 - 03:43] The reality of it, especially when you're sort of presenting the clients, that's sort of an indication that maybe the ranges here need to be tightened up.
[03:43 - 03:54] So maybe rather than doing 0.1, you might do something like 0.05 and kind of tighten up the ranges so that it just doesn't look as wide, right?
[03:54 - 03:59] Because ultimately, what are you saying if it can be anywhere in that range?
[03:59 - 04:07] So this is less about sort of substance and more about style that, you know, when you do present information like this, you tend to tighten it up.
[04:07 - 04:10] So even 200 to 500 as a range might be too high.

[04:33 - 04:42] If you did the perpetuity approach, the data table won't work because whatever exit multiple sensitivity point does not actually affect the perpetuity approach.
[04:42 - 04:45] So that's an important sort of mechanical thing when you're building a model.
[04:45 - 04:51] Well, so that a lot of times these sensitivity tables create confusion because of this, like you have to just be very careful.

### 45. DCF-Modeling-Negative-Net-Debt

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_DCF/DCF-Modeling-Negative-Net-Debt/transcript/DCF-Modeling-Negative-Net-Debt.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_DCF\DCF-Modeling-Negative-Net-Debt\transcript\DCF-Modeling-Negative-Net-Debt.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:24] As promised, when we calculated the WAC, I want to spend just a few more minutes addressing what I referred to as weirdness in the capital weights calculations, when we built Apple, if you recall, we calculated net debt, because net debt was a negative, it showed as a capital weight that's less than zero, and the market value of equity was greater than one.
[00:24 - 00:32] And so, this, in a way, bumps up the cost of equity from this understated 9.9 percent.
[00:32 - 00:40] And so, this, in a way, bumps up the cost of equity from this understated 9.9 percent.
[00:40 - 00:48] And because the company has so much cash, this actually understates the true riskiness of the cash flows that we're supposed to be valuing.
[00:48 - 01:02] And so, this, in a way, bumps up the cost of equity from this understated 9.9 percent to the truer cost of capital, which is higher than that by definition, because it's taking this sort of greater than one capital weight.
[01:02 - 01:17] So, I want to just kind of highlight that. I want to show simple example that explains why that's actually, why it's actually important that we have these capital weights that are greater than one for equity and less than zero for debt, when you have negative net debt, why that's actually a requirement.
[01:17 - 01:26] And I'll back up by saying that the whole thing is actually unnecessary if you use gross debt instead of net debt, but the problem with using gross debt is number one most people don't do it.
[01:26 - 01:32] And second, in terms of implementation, you have to be completely consistent if you're going to use gross debt in your capital weights.
[01:32 - 01:41] You're going to have to use gross debt in your calculation of beta, which we'll talk about why that's a little confusing and you just have to be pretty consistent about your approach.
[01:41 - 01:48] And so, let's talk about both approaches. Let's start with the common one, the one that we use and the one that you'll use most of the time, which is the net debt approach.
[01:48 - 01:53] So, we created a simplified WAC calculation simpler than Apple just so we can isolate the issue.
[01:53 - 01:57] And let's say the company that we're looking at the simple company has a risk-free rate of 2%?
[01:57 - 02:04] Marker is a premium of 6% and observed beta of 1% for a cost of equity of 8% pretty straightforward.
[02:04 - 02:17] Let's say it also has a cost of debt of 2% and no tax benefit from debt. In other words, let's ignore the tax effect, the tax deductibility of debt, so that the cost of debt is not tax affected, it just is 2%.
[02:17 - 02:23] You'll see what that makes it simpler to work with, it also creates a distortion that's unnecessary for us to focus on right now.
[02:23 - 02:31] So, equity, so let's say that the market value of the equity for the capital weight calculation purpose is 12,500.
[02:31 - 02:36] And let's say that the debt is 20, let's actually make this 200 and let's make cash 1,500.
[02:36 - 02:40] So, you have this large negative net debt position, just like we see with Apple.
[02:40 - 02:56] And what you see when you calculate the equity weight and the debt weight, just like we did with Apple, that if we define, you know, debt as net debt consistently, we take equity divided by equity plus this negative number and by definition, we're going to have a larger than one equity weight.
[02:56 - 03:06] And reverse is true as well. If we look at debt divided by that same figure, which is negative, plus equity, you're going to have something less than zero.
[03:06 - 03:18] And what that serves to do is when you actually calculate the weighted average cost of capital and you're using these weights, the cost of capital is actually greater than the cost of equity, which is unusual, right?
[03:18 - 03:28] Usually when you have normal equity weights that are less than one and debt weights that are greater than zero, you're going to have the cost of capital be less than the cost of equity because the cost of debt is usually less than the cost of equity.
[03:28 - 03:34] Here you have the reverse impact. We talked in that when we actually built the model, why conceptually that makes sense?
[03:34 - 03:47] That conceptually makes sense because the observed beta for a company that has large cash positions is actually lower than the true volatility of its cash flows.
[03:47 - 04:05] And that's because let's take Apple as a simple example. When you're investing in Apple, the riskiness to an investor in Apple associated with the cash flows in Apple is expected to generate in the future from iPhone sales, et cetera is mitigated by the fact that Apple has massive cash reserves.
[04:05 - 04:24] And so anytime the stock market goes up or down, let's say, and the stock market goes up by 1%, if it wasn't for the cash cushions that Apple benefits from, the cash flows in Apple, the forecast of Apple's cash flows may suffer, would suffer more in terms of the variability on the stock price.
[04:24 - 04:29] And so the observed beta is actually understated relative to the true riskiness of the cash flows.
[04:30 - 04:40] It's made up for by these capital weights. So whereas the cost of equity is only 8%, we're saying, hey, that's really not a true representation of the riskiness of the cash flows that we're forecasting.
[04:40 - 04:53] And because of the shielded by all this cash, whereas the weighted average cost of capital because it's sort of chewing up the equity weight gets you to a more accurate representation of what is the true cost of capital it's higher.
[04:54 - 05:08] Let's look at the, we could basically calculate a whack using a gross debt approach as well where the capital weights don't take into account net debt, but just look at the gross debt, effectively ignoring the cash altogether.
[05:08 - 05:15] This is a completely okay approach, and I'll show you that it leads to the exact same result as long as you're being consistent.
[05:15 - 05:21] Those folks don't use the gross debt approach. There's nothing wrong with it. It actually makes conceptually a little bit more sense.
[05:21 - 05:26] But I just want you to understand the sort of the equivalency between these two approaches.
[05:26 - 05:30] And so assume the same risk free rate, same market risk premium.
[05:30 - 05:36] The main difference is if we're using the equity weight that just takes equity, market value of equity divided by equity plus gross debt.
[05:36 - 05:41] And that weight is just gross debt divided by equity plus gross debt.
[05:41 - 05:45] That's okay, you no longer have this weirdness, you avoid the weirdness.
[05:45 - 05:57] But when it comes to calculating the cost of equity, to be consistent you have to calculate a beta that doesn't account for the cash that's sitting on the books.
[05:57 - 06:07] You have to calculate a cost of equity that is independent of the shows you what the beta would be if there wasn't all this cash on the books.
[06:07 - 06:14] So this might be a confusing term because you're not really unlevering for debt, but you were removing the effective cash.
[06:14 - 06:23] And the formula for that is you take the observed beta and you adjust it for how much cash the company actually has on its book.
[06:23 - 06:34] Here's the formula I've included it for you guys. This is how you get from an observed beta to a cash to a beta unaffected by cash.
[06:34 - 06:45] And now you would have to use that beta in order to be consistent because that gets your cost of equity to the right cost of equity if the company had no cash.
[06:45 - 06:55] And then you can use the gross debt weight. And notice by the way, the regardless of which approach you use, the cost of capital is exactly the same if you do this correctly.
[06:55 - 07:04] Just one fine point is we remove the tax rate. The tax rate does create a deviation between both approaches.
[07:04 - 07:15] And so that's why we removed it to show you that the two approaches are the same except for when you factor in the tax rate.
[07:15 - 07:17] That's why we left it off.
[07:17 - 07:31] So hopefully that clarifies the equivalency of both approaches and sort of settles in your mind that the negative debt weights and the greater than one equity weights in the net debt approach is perfectly correct and reasonable.
[07:31 - 07:37] Hopefully that even clarified a little bit what the observed beta represents and what it doesn't represent under both approaches.

[10:11 - 10:13] so they don't have to worry about that.
[10:13 - 10:16] And that there's no liquidation value.
[10:16 - 10:21] In other words, there's no value to the preferred shares that they get.
[10:21 - 10:25] It's either they're holding these preferred shares that are giving nothing to them,
[10:25 - 10:28] or they convert them into five shares of common stock,
[10:28 - 10:30] which obviously have that value.
[10:30 - 10:36] In this simple case, obviously, we need to factor in the fact that you have five times 15,
[10:36 - 10:41] 75 million potentially dilutive shares out there.
[10:41 - 10:45] Like these 15 million shares of convertible preferreds can become 75 million shares of common stock.
[10:45 - 10:49] So adding that to the denominator, you have the same numerator, right?
[10:49 - 10:50] The same equity value.
[10:50 - 10:53] That's defined based on the DCF we just built.
[10:53 - 10:56] The numerator, now that we found this disclosure,
[10:56 - 11:01] means that the real fair value for this company is not five, not four,
[11:01 - 11:04] but $2.50 per share, right?
[11:04 - 11:08] Again, it hopefully brings the point home that we have to consider all these potentially dilutive securities.

[03:07 - 03:10] Because they both have the same right to ownership
[03:11 - 03:16] We count them together. We just add them up even though in reality one class has more voting control than the other

### 51. DCF-Modeling-Terminal-Value---Exit-Multiple-Approach

[00:00 - 00:08] All right, so now that we've calculated the stage one free cash flows we turn to the terminal value. So first we're going to talk about
[00:08 - 00:10] the
[00:10 - 00:18] calculation of the terminal value using the growth and perpetuity approach. Now, this is the approach that we had originally talked about in some of the conceptual videos
[00:18 - 00:23] where we sort of assume that the company grows and perpetuities at a certain
[00:23 - 00:30] long-term growth rate, and then we're going to talk about another approach that is used as frequently if not more frequently
[00:30 - 00:35] in practice, which basically gets you to the same result, but in a slightly different way. So let's start with the growth and
[00:35 - 00:37] perpetuity approach. And
[00:37 - 00:42] and the most important thing that we have to assume is what do we think that beyond
[00:42 - 00:44] you know, beyond
[00:44 - 00:50] 2024, where the company is expected to generate unlevered free cash flows of 75 billion dollars,
[00:50 - 00:59] what is the rate at which the company's cash flows are going to grow beyond that? Remember the perpetuity approach requires that we make an assumption about the
[00:59 - 01:02] growth rate of those free cash flows in perpetuity. And so we're going to assume
[01:03 - 01:10] 3%, this is one of the key assumptions along with the discount rate in the DCF model. Anytime people talk about sort of the DCF
[01:10 - 01:17] you can make it say whatever you wanted to say, they'll point to the terminal growth calculation. And and theoretically this
[01:18 - 01:23] growth rate should represent the steady state growth rate that a company can sustain
[01:24 - 01:30] once it's competitive advantages and once it's reached sort of maturity, it's competitive and
[01:30 - 01:36] advantages start to narrow and it's reach maturity. And so a key feature of this is that you know,
[01:36 - 01:42] we're getting to that point where the company could sustain a growth rate forever. Now one of the
[01:42 - 01:46] one of the issues here is that you have kind of an upward bound on this. You can't say a company
[01:46 - 01:51] is going to grow at seven or eight percent in perpetuity. And that's because the expectation for
[01:51 - 01:58] the overall global economies growth rate is in the you know, the two to two to four percent range. And
[01:58 - 02:03] so you can't really say eight percent because with a magic of compound and you'll actually
[02:04 - 02:07] within a hundred years you'll be saying that the company's bigger than the global economy.
[02:08 - 02:13] And so you do have this constraint where basically all companies end up progressing into sort of a two
[02:13 - 02:19] to four, two to five percent long-term growth rate at the most with three being probably the most
[02:19 - 02:25] common one. And so every company gets like this three percent growth rate. But you know, that is
[02:25 - 02:31] the challenge of trying to predict what a company is going to look like, you know, 50 years from
[02:31 - 02:38] now or even six years from now. So the way we're going to calculate this is recall the formula
[02:38 - 02:43] that we introduced in the earlier concept videos where is you know, you have the growth in perpetuity
[02:43 - 02:51] formula. It says the value, the present value of a perpetuity today. So present value of time zero
[02:52 - 03:01] equals the free cash flow at time one divided by the discount rate. In this case, it's the
[03:01 - 03:07] whack minus that long-term growth rate. Notice this is at time one. So the present value of time zero
[03:07 - 03:11] is the next period's free cash flow. And so that's exactly what we're going to do here. Well, what is
[03:11 - 03:19] you know, we want to discount this, you know, 2025 and beyond, right? So we want to get it to
[03:19 - 03:24] to two step process. We're going to get the present value as a September 30 2024. So in order to do
[03:24 - 03:33] that, we take the September 30 2024 cash flow grow up by one plus that three percent assumption
[03:33 - 03:40] because we need that free cash flow in the next period. So 2025, right? And then we divide that

[03:49 - 03:53] important that we understand what this is going to get us. When I hit enter here, you're going to get

[04:12 - 04:18] All right, so we still need to discount that back to the present. And the present is the valuation
[04:18 - 04:24] date. So we're going to use the same sort of mechanics that we have used up till now. And that

### 53. DCF-Modeling-The-2-Stage-DCF-Approach

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_DCF/DCF-Modeling-The-2-Stage-DCF-Approach/transcript/DCF-Modeling-The-2-Stage-DCF-Approach.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:05] The DCF will often be comprised of really what's called two stages.
[00:05 - 00:09] The first stage, being an explicit forecast period,
[00:09 - 00:16] much like we just had that exercise where we tried to figure out what is the present value of the Houtok stand for the first five years.
[00:16 - 00:22] So you'll have an explicit forecast period, and that's consistent for those of you who just completed a financial statement model,
[00:22 - 00:29] where if you recall, we forecast five years worth of Apple financials,
[00:29 - 00:36] five years worth of Apple forecasts. And so we would have five years of Apple cash flows.
[00:36 - 00:40] And we can discount that to the present.
[00:40 - 00:46] And then the second stage would be a stage that looks at all cash flows beyond 2019.
[00:46 - 00:52] Of course, remember the assumption in the DCF, the implicit assumption is that a business continues to operate indefinitely.
[00:52 - 00:57] It would actually be more strange to sort of assume that at some point it just stops operating.
[00:57 - 01:04] And so then the next stage is calculating some perpetual growth stage,
[01:04 - 01:07] where you just, you no longer look at explicit cash flows.
[01:07 - 01:14] You no longer the idea is you no longer have any real explicit thesis on what the cash flows are going to be.
[01:14 - 01:22] But rather you sort of throw up a blanket statement that says, okay, well, you know, if I finish my explicit forecast period at $20,500,
[01:22 - 01:25] let me just grow that every year by 5% indefinitely.
[01:25 - 01:28] Let's slap on that 10% perpetual discount rate.
[01:28 - 01:35] And then everything can sort of derive into or regress into that perpetuity formula that we just introduced.
[01:35 - 01:37] So why don't we go ahead and do that?
[01:37 - 01:39] Let's build a mini DCF.
[01:39 - 01:45] Again, we still haven't defined who these cash flows belong to or who the discount rate belongs to.
[01:45 - 01:47] That's actually going to be the next step after this.
[01:47 - 01:55] But for now, let's build the two stage DCF based on the forecast that I've outlined here.
[01:55 - 02:01] And assuming, again, that all cash flows are generated at the very end of the period that's going to make discounting a little simpler.
[02:01 - 02:05] What's the present value of the hot dog stand with this information?
[02:05 - 02:09] So why don't you hit the pause button, give this a shot, and then we'll go through it together as a group.
[02:09 - 02:12] Okay.
[02:12 - 02:18] So here we have a mini discounted cash flow analysis.
[02:18 - 02:20] And we have laid out the cash flows.
[02:20 - 02:28] Notice we have laid it out horizontally now consistent with the way we build our models with the forecast increasing from left to right.
[02:28 - 02:32] And we have our five periods with our cash flows.
[02:32 - 02:43] The first stage we discount each cash flow by the discount rate.
[02:43 - 02:45] And here is the formula.
[02:45 - 02:54] You basically take each cash flow divided by one plus that 10% discount rate and raise it to the period that you're in.
[02:54 - 03:05] The next stage, after you've added all of this up, this represents the total present value of cash flows through 2019.
[03:05 - 03:11] But how do you get the present value of the hot dog stand beyond 2019?
[03:11 - 03:17] Well, the next step is to take that final year cash flow of 2500.
[03:17 - 03:28] Remember that the perpetuity formula requires that we take the next year's cash flow 2020 cash flow divided by the discount rate minus the perpetual growth rate.
[03:28 - 03:33] That'll get you the present value of all cash flows beyond 2019.
[03:33 - 03:37] That'll get you the present value of that in 2019.
[03:37 - 03:39] So that's a nuance.
[03:39 - 03:41] So you have to take the next year cash flow.
[03:41 - 03:45] And if we're going to assume a perpetual growth rate of 5%, we can actually already take advantage of that.
[03:45 - 03:49] And grow 2500 by one plus G.
[03:49 - 03:53] That gets us to this 21,525.
[03:53 - 03:56] We divide that by the 10% minus the 5%.
[03:56 - 03:58] That's the growth and perpetuity formula.
[03:58 - 04:06] That gives you the present value in 2019 of all cash flows expected beyond 2019.
[04:06 - 04:10] But of course, we don't care about present values as of 2019.
[04:10 - 04:12] We've been discounting to 2015.
[04:12 - 04:15] The date of our valuation is the beginning of 2015.
[04:15 - 04:20] So we get to discount this 430 all the way back to 2015.
[04:20 - 04:28] And the way we do that is we're going to take this 430 and we're going to divide that by one plus the discount rate.
[04:28 - 04:31] I didn't know what that is are.
[04:31 - 04:37] We're going to raise that to the periods that we want to go back, which is 5 periods.
[04:37 - 04:40] And that gets us to 267, 306.
[04:40 - 04:43] That's the present value of that perpetuity value.
[04:43 - 04:46] Another term for that is the present value of the terminal value.
[04:46 - 04:52] And so it's this that we add to the sum of all of these present values.
[04:52 - 04:58] And if we did our numbers right, we should get 323,547.
[04:58 - 05:07] So this is more true to a real DCF in terms of the discounting that's taking place between the two stages.
[05:07 - 05:12] So I'm going to open up Excel and just show you how I did this formulaically in Excel as well.
[05:12 - 05:20] I've included the Excel file that does this exercise in your files as well.
[05:20 - 05:21] Here we go.
[05:21 - 05:24] So just so we formulaically understand what we're doing here.
[05:24 - 05:29] We take the cash flow that we laid out, the blue represents it's a hard-coded number.
[05:29 - 05:33] Take one plus the discount rate.
[05:33 - 05:36] We anchor that because it's going to be the same discount rate every time.
[05:36 - 05:41] And to make it easy, we have included the period above here.
[05:41 - 05:49] So you could just reference that copy and paste that across and you get these figures.
[05:49 - 05:55] The 2020 cash flow is simply the 2019 cash flow times one plus the growth rate.
[05:55 - 06:01] We take that 2020 cash flow divided by the discount rate minus the growth rate.
[06:01 - 06:10] We discount that by five periods because we wanted to represent the present value in 2015, not 2019.
[06:10 - 06:18] And then we take the present value of the terminal value and add all the present values of the cash flows in the explicit forecast period.
[06:18 - 06:24] And we get the first sort of basic DCF exercise under our belts.

[05:56 - 06:01] going on here, right, cat-backs? We're basically growing cat-backs with sales. By anchoring
[06:01 - 06:06] cat-backs to a percentage of revenue, we're saying it'll grow with revenue. Let's see if that's
[06:06 - 06:14] in line with what the analysts are saying. They're saying it'll end by 2024 at 13.1. This is maybe,
[06:14 - 06:21] so it's a little bit aggressive maybe. So maybe we bring this down just a tad. Maybe we bring this
[06:21 - 06:37] down to 3.9, 3.8, 3.7, 3.6. And that gets us much closer in line to the consensus.

### 56. DCF-Modeling-Understanding-Net-Debt

[00:00 - 00:08] Now that we've calculated enterprise value, the next step is to subtract net debt from enterprise value to arrive at an equity value.
[00:08 - 00:15] This is really the last step before we can figure out what the equity value and then the equity value per share of the business.
[00:15 - 00:27] So, from a real world implementation standpoint, we want to use the book values of the net debt components as of the latest filing,
[00:27 - 00:33] as proxies for the market value, unless you're instructed otherwise or have specific reasons to do otherwise.
[00:33 - 00:40] And what I mean by that is if we sort of think about net debt as being comprised of debt and equivalence net of non-operating assets,
[00:40 - 00:46] and the most common debt in equivalence are straight plain debt, non-controlling interest and preferred stock.
[00:46 - 00:50] And we'll talk about sort of these other two less clear ones in just a bit.
[00:50 - 00:59] But then we also have cash and other non-operating assets as the non-operating assets that need to be netted out of debt to get a net debt position.
[00:59 - 01:18] Well, the reason why technically this actually needs to be reflected when you're making adjustments down to get equity value using market values is because ultimately the value that you as the equity owner get to sort of pocket if you were to imagine sell the business or if you're just trying to value the business on a standalone basis.
[01:18 - 01:26] Well, if the market values of the debt that you have outstanding or the market value that cash that you have on your balance sheet is different from the actual book value,
[01:26 - 01:32] well then certainly the market value is what ultimately determines what your value as the residual equity owner is.
[01:32 - 01:40] But of course cash, for example, is almost always has the exact same market value as the book value.
[01:41 - 01:44] Debt is actually very similar.
[01:44 - 01:50] It's very rare that the market value of debt is significantly different than its book value although far less rather than cash.
[01:50 - 02:02] There are many times in distressed debt situations or situations where the credit environment has changed dramatically where the market values do end up being a little bit different from the book values.
[02:02 - 02:08] But 99 out of 100 times you're going to be just fine using book values as of the latest filing.
[02:08 - 02:31] So if the company that you're looking at, if Apple has the most recent 10 Q, the most recent filing is the 10 Q just go to the most recent 10 Q or for the most recent filing is the 10 K you can go to the most recent 10 K and find the latest debt balances, the latest not controlling interest balances, the latest preferred stock balances, the latest cash and equivalence, the latest other non operating assets and use those book values as a proxy for market value.
[02:33 - 02:37] Now, let's talk about the various components of net debt in a little bit more detail.
[02:37 - 02:48] We up till now have sort of talked about the basic logic of why we need to subtract debt from enterprise value to figure out how much equity value remains.
[02:48 - 02:55] And then of course we need to add any excess cash in non operating assets to really figure out exactly how much equity owners put in their pockets.
[02:55 - 02:59] But in terms of the individual components of debt, let's talk about that now.
[03:00 - 03:20] So first we know that we want to use the latest book value of debt, specifically what you're going to see when you look at a 10 K or 10 Q is you're going to see debt bifurcated into several sort of items and you're going to see something called current portion of long term debt, short term debt, long term debt, you're also going to have convertible debt as well potentially for some companies.
[03:20 - 03:31] We want all of those line items and again barring some deviation from market values to book values, book value is going to be just fine most of the time to use straight from the 10 from the 10 Q or the 10 came.
[03:31 - 03:42] Now at the risk of getting maybe perhaps a little too into the weeds here, when we go and do our case study and we calculate net debt in just a moment for Apple, we're going to use the balance sheet balances for debt.
[03:42 - 04:00] Now the one thing to keep in mind is actually to be 100% rigorous, understand that the balance sheet provides debt net of any unamortized debt discounts and debt issuance costs and that's just generally accepted accounting principles for how you recognize debt on the balance sheet.
[04:00 - 04:09] So if you issued a $10 billion note or a $1 billion note, whatever, and you have to pay fees, debt issuance cost rate, you have to pay financing fees for that.
[04:09 - 04:18] So what you're going to recognize on the balance sheet is debt net of those fees whereas what we actually want is the total outstanding principle.
[04:18 - 04:27] And so usually this is sort of a non material sort of thing which is why we kind of described it as getting into the weeds.
[04:27 - 04:33] But when it is material, you always want to take a glance and see whether there's a footnote disclosure.
[04:33 - 04:41] Usually after the balance sheet there's a footnote disclosure that tells you what is the principle balance and that's actually the peripheral bowl thing to use.
[04:41 - 04:45] Again, we're not going to even get into it because it's fairly immaterial for Apple.

[04:56 - 05:06] So here you have about $280 million worth of debt and then if you look at the balance sheet, you look at these two components.
[05:06 - 05:08] Here it was actually a little bit material.
