[05:47 - 05:51] Now, the one thing I want to do is I want to get back to that prior slide and talk about convertible debt.
[05:51 - 05:55] It's not going to happen very often, but you might see convertible debt on a company's balance sheet.
[05:55 - 05:59] Convertible debt, of course, can be converted by definition to common stock.
[05:59 - 06:14] And so if you're going to assume conversion for the purposes of calculating shares outstanding, we're going to get to that section in terms of how do you decide whether convertible debt or convertible preferred stock needs to be converted
[06:14 - 06:19] in order, or should that be included in the diluted share calculation in just a moment.
[06:19 - 06:26] One of our next videos is going to be talking about whether do we assume conversion of these liabilities or not.
[06:26 - 06:32] But we're just going to jump ahead a little bit and say if we're ultimately going to assume that conversion is going to take place.
[06:32 - 06:39] Well, then you have to be very careful not to include convertible debt in the net debt balance.
[06:39 - 06:41] Otherwise, you'd be double counting.
[06:41 - 06:48] Imagine if you assume conversion and now you have a much higher share base to divide your equity value by.
[06:48 - 07:04] Well, then if you were to also include the pre-converted convertible debt balance in net debt, you'd be double counting the hit that this financial obligation has in terms of the equity value of the business.
[07:04 - 07:09] So if we're going to assume conversion, do not include convertible debt in the net debt calculation.
[07:09 - 07:15] If you don't assume conversion for the purposes of diluted shares outstanding, then you should include it in the net debt calculation.
[07:15 - 07:24] So if you have preferred stock that isn't convertible to common shares, it should absolutely be included in net debt.
[07:24 - 07:25] No questions asked.
[07:25 - 07:29] Again, the issue there is do we use book values?
[07:29 - 07:31] The answer is most of the time.
[07:31 - 07:36] Yes, there may be situations in which the market value of preferred stock is significantly higher than the book value.
[07:36 - 07:41] In that case, if you have any information on what the market value is, incorporate that.
[07:41 - 07:47] But that notwithstanding, you're going to use the book value if you have no information otherwise.
[07:47 - 07:53] The issue is the same for convertible preferred stock as it is for convertible debt.
[07:53 - 07:59] And that is if you have convertible preferred stock before you just put it in the net debt calculation.
[07:59 - 08:06] You have to understand whether you are going to assume conversion for the purposes of calculating diluted shares.
[08:06 - 08:17] If you're going to assume conversion, then it is no longer a financial obligation that needs to be accounted for in the net debt side because it's being accounted for in the diluted share count.
[08:17 - 08:24] The one thing to keep in mind for those that are sort of thinking about why do I have to include preferred stock in net debt in the first place.
[08:24 - 08:33] Remember that ultimately the transition to get from enterprise value to equity value is the transition where you start with enterprise value.
[08:33 - 08:37] You add anything, any non operating assets like cash.
[08:37 - 08:49] And then you subtract that all non common equity financial obligations from the business because we're trying to figure out ultimately what goes into the pockets of the common equity owners.
[08:49 - 08:56] And so preferred stock is certainly a financial claim against the business that isn't a common equity claim.
[08:56 - 09:06] And that leads us nicely to the other non common equity claim against the business that we have to think about in net debt and that's non controlling interest.
[09:06 - 09:12] So the value of the business that belongs to non controlling interest should be included in net debt.
[09:12 - 09:21] It does not belong to common equity holders. It is a financial claim against the business that needs to therefore be excluded from the equity value or taken out of the equity value.
[09:22 - 09:25] We're going to want to use the latest book value against same caveat applies.
[09:25 - 09:35] If you have any reason to believe that the non controlling interest have a value in the business that is significantly greater than the book value, then you're going to want to try to understand what that value is.
[09:35 - 09:43] The implementation challenges are that it's very difficult to really ultimillion understand what the market value is of non controlling interest.
[09:43 - 09:48] There are certain methodologies that are a little outside the scope of this program to figure that out.
[09:48 - 09:57] And like with several of the things that we've been looking at, 99 out of 100 times the book value is used as a proxy for market value.
[09:57 - 10:01] Just be aware that technically it is the market value that should be taken out.
[10:01 - 10:05] Again, latest 10K, latest 10Q incorporates the non controlling it.
[10:05 - 10:11] We'll show you the non controlling interest balance on the balance sheet if the company has a significant amount of non controlling interest.
[10:11 - 10:19] The one thing to be clear on is sometimes a source of confusion is you see non controlling interest both on the income statement and on the balance sheet as a line item.
[10:19 - 10:21] What we want to take out here is the balance.
[10:21 - 10:28] It represents the actual value of non controlling interest as opposed to non controlling interest expense.
[10:28 - 10:39] The second sort of related piece of that is when you're looking at a company's income statement and you see non controlling interest expense, that needs to be excluded from the calculation of unlevered free cash flows.
[10:39 - 10:50] The nice thing is usually you don't have to deal with that because if you start with unlevered free cash flow calculations with EBIT, if EBIT is your starting line, that's before non controlling interest expense.
[10:50 - 10:58] Typically, most companies recognize not controlling interest expense below EBIT is a non operating expense.
[10:58 - 11:02] And so you avoid having to manually exclude anything.
[11:02 - 11:06] We don't have any non controlling interest for Apple, and so it's not much of an issue.
[11:06 - 11:20] But those that have gone through our advanced accounting course and have a firm handle on sort of non controlling interest accounting understand that the non controlling interest expense is presented below EBIT and that it's recognized as an equity item.
[11:20 - 11:24] It's sort of this mezzanine equity liability is account on the balance sheet.
[11:24 - 11:33] And every year that the non controlling interest expense is recognized on the income statement that mezzanine equity account grows and grows and grows.
[11:33 - 11:45] So from a DCF perspective, understand that the key consistent issue has to be that non controlling interest expense should not be counted in the calculation of unlevered free cash flow.
[11:45 - 11:54] And instead, the book value of it needs to be taken into account in the net debt calculation.
[11:54 - 11:56] Now we turn to non operating assets.
[11:56 - 12:04] So the cash flows that are related to non operating assets, interest income, we're not reflecting in our free cash flow calculation.
[12:04 - 12:11] Remember, since we started with EBIT earnings before interest in taxes, that is before interest income just like before interest expense.
[12:11 - 12:22] And of course, for Apple, there's a lot of interest income. They're generating a lot of interest income from all of their idle cash, all the cash that they've put into investments and the like.
[12:22 - 12:33] The most important thing to stress and I'll repeat it is that the free cash flow calculation that we built by starting with EBIT avoids accounting for that in the free cash flows.
[12:33 - 12:35] And that's by design.
[12:35 - 12:48] It's because we recognize the value of the non operating assets of the business, not by discounting them, not by discounting the interest income that they generate to the present.
[12:48 - 12:59] But rather by simply looking at the current book value of those non operating assets, just like debt, and netting that against the gross debt for this business.
[12:59 - 13:10] And that's because we want to separate out operating assets, operating liabilities, those are the things that end up creating unlevered free cash flows that we do discount to the present.
[13:10 - 13:18] And we want to separate from that non operating liabilities like debt, preferred stock and non controlling interest or financial liabilities as we call them.
[13:18 - 13:22] And non operating assets, cash, we don't need to build a DCF for cash.
[13:22 - 13:29] It's pretty clear that if you have $150 billion of cash sitting on the balance sheet, that is $150 billion in value.
[13:29 - 13:37] We don't need to build a DCF for that the way we have to build for the operating assets and liabilities that are sitting on the company's balance sheet.
[13:37 - 13:48] Again, the book value of the idle cash and investments, you can find in the latest 10Q and 10K, and that is virtually always quite comparable to the market value of those securities.

[01:38 - 01:44] And your business is capitalized by both debt and equity.
[01:44 - 01:50] And you've got some payments, like interest payments that have to be made to the lenders.
[01:50 - 02:00] Well, the unlevered free cash flow really represents your core operating cash flows to the enterprise before accounting for any of those payments.
[02:00 - 02:06] Whereas the levered free cash flow, let's say you have $200 worth of payments you have to make to lenders.
[02:06 - 02:11] That means that only 800 of the operating cash flows.
[02:11 - 02:19] In other words, after accounting for the 200 in interest expense really are available now to be distributed to equity owners.
[02:19 - 02:28] So because the lenders are first in line, they get paid out first and levered represents what remains to the equity owners.
[02:28 - 02:35] So think about this as the undistributed cash flows, undistributed to any provider of capital,
[02:35 - 02:43] levered free cash flows are cash flows that are distributed, that are after having distributed to the lenders, the debt providers' capital.
[02:43 - 02:45] This is what's left for the equity owners.

[00:00 - 00:13] In this video, I want to have a somewhat more theoretical discussion than what we've been doing up to now in terms of the modeling and that is to try to dig a little deeper into some of the formulas we've been using and understanding what are the underlying drivers of those formulas.
[00:13 - 00:27] So if you recall, we have a formula that the perpetuity formula is defined as free cash flows in the next period divided by the discount rate minus the growth rate and that gave you the value of a company, so that's a key formula in all of our evaluations.
[00:27 - 00:34] But the question that I want to address in this video is how do companies actually generate the growth that's used in that equation?
[00:34 - 00:40] How do the companies reinvestment decisions and returns on capital affect value?

[00:49 - 00:57] The first is the operating profit generated during the period and that's for the purposes of simplicity here.

[00:00 - 00:05] So now let's talk about the weighted average cost of capital in a little bit more detail.
[00:05 - 00:13] We walk through the calculation of WAC fairly in a fairly formulaic way when we built our model,
[00:13 - 00:21] but WAC becomes a very important part of the model and understanding the elements of the discount rate

[00:28 - 00:32] have gone through this in corporate finance, either our corporate finance course or in school.
[00:32 - 00:37] And so I'm going to go through this really in the context of how it affects our modeling exercise,
[00:37 - 00:41] but some conceptual logic has to be put in place first. So let's go through that first.
[00:42 - 00:48] Understand that both debt and equity investors contribute capital to businesses
[00:48 - 00:53] with the expectation that the risk that they take will be offset by an appropriate return.
[00:53 - 00:58] And quantifying that cost of capital is different for both debt and equity. For debt providers or
[00:58 - 01:03] capital, it's relatively straightforward to understand the cost of debt, because ultimately,
[01:03 - 01:08] the lenders charge an interest rate, there's an interest rate associated with it. And the
[01:08 - 01:14] interest rate really plays a big part in quantifying the cost of debt, specifically the yield
[01:14 - 01:18] that's a function of what the interest rate is and what the current value market value of the debt
[01:18 - 01:23] is really informs the cost of debt for a company. There's also credit rating agencies that provide
[01:23 - 01:27] further insight. This is straightforward as we'll see in a second. The cost of equity is incredibly
[01:27 - 01:32] challenging and there is a lot of debate about what is the way to calculate that. So we talked
[01:32 - 01:38] about this formula. This is the weighted average cost of capital formula. It basically defines
[01:38 - 01:43] the cost of debt and the cost of equity or it sort of frames the cost of debt and the cost of
[01:43 - 01:49] equity as an awaited average basis where you look at the total capital structure and you say
[01:49 - 01:54] the fraction of the capital structure that's funded with debt, we multiply that percentage by
[01:54 - 02:01] the cost of debt and conversely, we look at the fraction of the capital structure that's funded
[02:01 - 02:07] with equity and we multiply that by the cost of equity. The one other adjustment in the cost of
[02:07 - 02:13] debt side of this equation is that because the cost of debt as we now know, it actually provides
[02:13 - 02:18] is actually a little bit lower than the nominal cost of debt. If the interest, if the interest
[02:18 - 02:23] rates say for a company is 10%, but the company faces a 40% tax rate, the fact that it gets to
[02:23 - 02:30] shield itself from taxes because it carries this interest expense means that we want to adjust
[02:30 - 02:38] the actual cost of debt down to reflect that there is a tax shield. So that's the formula and now
[02:38 - 02:43] just talking about some of these elements, let's talk about the easy elements first or some of the
[02:43 - 02:48] more straightforward elements, is to understand that for a DCF to really be effective, we need to
[02:48 - 02:56] assume a constant weighted average cost of capital throughout. And so DCFs are rarely implemented
[02:56 - 03:02] where there is a different whack for each year. The whack is assumed to be constant throughout.
[03:03 - 03:08] At worst, there are two different whacks, whacks in stage one and whack in stage two, but even that's
[03:08 - 03:15] fairly rare. We assume a constant whack throughout because there are implications, if you assume a
[03:15 - 03:20] changing whack every year, you're going to have to assume changing net debt relationships to
[03:20 - 03:24] unlever free cash flows. And so implementation becomes pretty difficult. So we assume a constant whack
[03:24 - 03:33] throughout. And so the implicit assumption there is that the DCF reflects a value where the company
[03:33 - 03:39] maintains a stable capital structure. By the way, the capital structure doesn't have to be, for the
[03:39 - 03:44] purposes of forecasting, doesn't have to be the same as what it currently is historically. It can
[03:44 - 03:48] be different. So for example, if you're building a DCF in the context of an acquisition and you're
[03:48 - 03:53] saying, you know, I think in the context of this acquisition, the company will take on more debt or
[03:53 - 03:59] less debt. That's fine. It just has to be consistent from the moment that you begin your forecasting.
[03:59 - 04:05] And so if that assumption isn't really viable, if you believe that the capital structure
[04:06 - 04:11] will change and you have specific sort of expectations for how that will change. And when
[04:11 - 04:17] you're going to need to adjust your whack for each period and therefore in that case, there are
[04:17 - 04:23] models, specifically the adjusted present value or the APV model is really designed to facilitate
[04:23 - 04:27] different whacks through each period. And so the DCF becomes less relevant when that's the case.
[04:28 - 04:33] Just understand that the DCF is usually more than okay, because most companies do maintain
[04:33 - 04:39] pretty stable and manage to a fairly stable capital structure. So let's talk about these
[04:39 - 04:45] equity capital and debt capital weights. So in just kind of going back, we're going to focus on
[04:45 - 04:52] on these on this section first. And how do we figure out what is the equity value of a business?
[04:52 - 04:57] So first understand that the equity weight in the whack calculation, we want to use for public
[04:57 - 05:02] companies, the easiest thing to do is to just use the market cap of the business. So if we're
[05:02 - 05:07] valuing Apple, all we need is to look at the, the ludicrous outstanding for Apple times the
[05:07 - 05:12] Apple share price. And that'll be used as the equity capital weight for the purposes of
[05:12 - 05:16] calculating whack. For private companies, it's a little trickier. You're going to do a lot of DCFs
[05:16 - 05:23] in your careers for private companies. So how do we do that? Well, one very simple option is to use
[05:23 - 05:30] the equity value that we are deriving from the DCF. So ultimately, the DCF provides us with an equity
[05:30 - 05:35] value. We can use that equity value in turn to figure out what the implied whack is. Of course,
[05:35 - 05:40] that creates a circularity. That's, that is a fundamentally circular reasoning because the whack
[05:40 - 05:46] is used to derive the very equity value that we are then using in the whack calculation. If we do
[05:46 - 05:52] that, we can certainly do that and Excel will find a steady state. But you have to be sure that
[05:52 - 05:56] just like with a financial statement model, where we have an intentional circularity for interest
[05:56 - 06:02] expense and interest income, make sure that iterations are selected in Excel and insert a circuit
[06:02 - 06:07] breaker and just as a reminder of what that's all about. Let me show you the iteration settings
[06:07 - 06:20] in Excel. Alt T O. Oops. Alt T O opens up general formulas. We want to make sure that this is
[06:20 - 06:25] selected if you're going to create an intentional circularity ever in your model. Otherwise, it'll,
[06:25 - 06:31] it'll throw up an error. The debt weight, most of the time you can simply use the book value of
[06:31 - 06:37] debt as an approximation for the market value of debt. Just like we use the book value of debt
[06:37 - 06:42] for calculating net debt as a proxy, we can do the same thing for calculating the debt weight in
[06:42 - 06:47] the whack calculation. More rarely. And just like with the net debt calculation, more rarely,
[06:47 - 06:52] if interest rates have changed substantially since the debt issuance, meaning that the market
[06:52 - 06:58] values of the debt are different than the book values of the debt. You want to use the market
[06:58 - 07:02] price of the company's debt. And again, you can use Bloomberg and you can use some of these
[07:02 - 07:06] other resources to try to identify what the market value of the debt is. Obviously, Bloomberg
[07:06 - 07:10] being the most, the most obvious choice for understanding market values of debt.
[07:11 - 07:16] So now we've talked about the, the capital weights and just kind of going back, make sure you
[07:16 - 07:21] understand we have now talked about this piece. How to calculate that? And we've talked about this
[07:21 - 07:28] piece. The next section we're going to talk about, how do we estimate this piece? So the cost
[07:28 - 07:35] of debt for public, for companies that have public debt is, it's directly observable in the market
[07:35 - 07:40] as the current yield to maturity is on the company's long term debt. And so if the company doesn't
[07:40 - 07:45] have a very complicated capital structure with multiple tranches of the debt where they're senior
[07:45 - 07:50] debt and subordinated debt. And so where the different tranches of debt have dramatically
[07:50 - 07:56] different interest rates, if you have a relatively straightforward capital structure and the majority
[07:56 - 08:02] of your debt comes from this public debt that's directly observable in the market. So like a bond
[08:02 - 08:07] offering that is, that is public, you can go to Bloomberg and you can see what is the current
[08:07 - 08:13] yield to maturity on that long term debt. That's probably the best cost of debt when that information
[08:13 - 08:19] is available. Understand that analysts also instead frequently use the, just the coupon rate
[08:19 - 08:24] of the company's long term debt, but that's dangerous for the same reasons that we talked about
[08:24 - 08:28] of using the book value as a proxy for debt when the interest rate environment is, is changed.
[08:28 - 08:32] It's dangerous to use the coupon rate if it's significantly different from the current
[08:32 - 08:37] yield to maturity because the current yield to maturity actually does reflect what the company's
[08:37 - 08:43] true cost would be if it went out there and try to borrow in the future. Where's the coupon rate
[08:43 - 08:48] is a fundamentally historical rate and could be different, especially if the company issues their
[08:48 - 08:53] debt at a discount or at some sort of premium. And so the coupon rate is actually just, usually it
[08:53 - 08:57] works, but it doesn't always and doesn't fundamentally have to work as opposed to the
[08:57 - 09:03] yield to maturity. For private companies or companies where there is no public debt or observable

[09:09 - 09:15] rating agencies like Moody's and S&P provide yield spreads over US treasury by credit rating.
[09:15 - 09:20] And so whatever the company's credit rating is, assuming the company is rated, you can simply
[09:20 - 09:26] access Moody's and S&P yield spreads which are published by these folks directly and then distributed
[09:26 - 09:31] through services like Bloomberg, et cetera, to provide yield spreads over the US treasury.
[09:32 - 09:39] Understand that the all else equal, the more debt weighted the capital structure is,
[09:39 - 09:44] all else equal, the higher the cost of debt will be because obviously the more debt you have,
[09:44 - 09:50] the more levered you are, the more highly, the higher the default risk is going to be. And so if,
[09:50 - 09:55] for example, you are now expecting this company to undergo a more highly levered capital structure
[09:55 - 09:59] because you're building a DCF in the context of an acquisition where perhaps there's going to

[10:37 - 10:43] calculation. And we've done that because we are going to account for those benefits here in the
[10:43 - 10:50] weighted average cost of capital calculation. Specifically, we account for it by tax adjusting the
[10:50 - 10:55] cost of debt. And here, this is the example that we've sort of alluded to in the past, which is
[10:55 - 11:03] imagine a $10 million loan at a 10% interest rate at a 40% tax rate that cost of debt is not 10%,
[11:03 - 11:08] but it's 6%, that's because for every dollar of interest expense, that's going to reduce earnings
[11:08 - 11:14] by a dollar. But then pre-tax earnings by dollar, but of course it's going to reduce our tax bill
[11:14 - 11:22] by 40 cents, one minus the marginal tax rate at a marginal tax rate of 40%, that means we only
[11:22 - 11:28] trickle down 60 cents. The cost of the borrowing is only really 60 cents. So we're saving 40 cents
[11:28 - 11:32] on the dollar because of the tax deductibility of interest expense. Now one thing that I do want to

[11:40 - 11:48] that tax deductibility, especially in the first several years of the DCF, this might need to be lowered,
[11:48 - 11:56] that the tax benefits only exist if you can actually take advantage of them.

[00:00 - 00:06] Now, we turn to the far more complicated calculation, and that is the cost of equity.
[00:06 - 00:10] Now, understand that the cost of equity, unlike the cost of debt, is not directly observed
[00:10 - 00:11] in the market.
[00:11 - 00:16] Whereas the yield to maturity for a public debt is directly observable, and that represents
[00:16 - 00:17] our cost of debt.
[00:17 - 00:21] With cost of equity, you can't look at what Google share price is and somehow understand
[00:21 - 00:25] what the implied cost of equity is.
[00:25 - 00:26] You can't look at Apple or any of these companies.
[00:26 - 00:28] You see their share price.
[00:28 - 00:30] That's directly observable in the market.
[00:30 - 00:34] The equity value per shares directly observable in the market, but certainly not the cost
[00:34 - 00:37] of capital.
[00:37 - 00:43] So understanding that we don't have the same sort of directly observable in the market
[00:43 - 00:50] that we do with debt, we need to start thinking about how do we estimate the cost of equity.
[00:50 - 00:55] First, we need to understand and remember that the cost of equity, in the same way that
[00:55 - 01:00] the cost of debt represents the expected rate of return for debt investors, for lenders,
[01:00 - 01:05] the cost of equity represents the expected rate of return for equity investors.
[01:05 - 01:10] And that expected rate of return is fundamentally correlated with risk.
[01:10 - 01:17] The more risk you perceive, the higher your required and expected return will be.
[01:17 - 01:18] And how is that quantified?
[01:18 - 01:20] That's the main question.
[01:20 - 01:25] And so the methodology that is prevalent is to start with a risk-free rate, understanding
[01:25 - 01:31] that we really think about the expected rate of return for equity investing in equities
[01:31 - 01:36] is a class, is going to start with a certain baseline, which is the risk-free rate.
[01:36 - 01:41] I will expect some sort of premium over the rate that I would expect of just putting
[01:41 - 01:46] my money in a risk-free security.
[01:46 - 01:50] And so you have the sort of the time value of money as the first baseline, which is I would
[01:50 - 01:55] obviously prefer money today than having that money a year from now, then locking it up
[01:55 - 01:59] for a year, and that's just the time value, even if it's a risk-free security.
[01:59 - 02:04] So let's start with that rate and then try to understand a premium that is specific to
[02:04 - 02:10] equity as a class and also specific to the company that we're valuing.
[02:10 - 02:13] So first of all, understand that there are multiple models that exist for estimating the
[02:13 - 02:14] cost of equity.
[02:15 - 02:19] The model that we're going to talk about is it just happens to be the most widely used,
[02:19 - 02:24] but it's one of multiple models that are out there in academia to try to price equity.
[02:24 - 02:28] So the models that we've laid out here, the Fama French model, the arbitrary pricing theory
[02:28 - 02:33] model, and last but not least, the CAPM model, the one that we're going to talk about,
[02:33 - 02:36] these are all ways to try to estimate that cost of equity.
[02:36 - 02:41] The CAPM being the most widely used also happens to be the most often criticized model,
[02:41 - 02:45] effectively divides risk into two pieces.
[02:45 - 02:50] The first risk is something that they call unsystematic or company-specific risk,
[02:50 - 02:55] and that's risk that can be diversified away, and so the idea is ignore this risk altogether.
[02:55 - 02:56] What does that mean?
[02:56 - 03:01] So if you're trying to invest in, if you're looking to invest in Apple,
[03:01 - 03:07] the idea that something company-specific with Apple might go wrong, something, you know,
[03:07 - 03:13] so the fact that Steve Jobs' departure was obviously a potential risk for Apple,
[03:14 - 03:18] before he left, those were risks that were being addressed.
[03:18 - 03:23] Those are company-specific risks, and effectively the CAPM model says ignore those risks.
[03:23 - 03:24] Why?
[03:24 - 03:30] Because you can ignore those risks, you can diversify those risks away.
[03:31 - 03:35] In other words, by having the ability to diversify, you can invest not just in Apple,
[03:35 - 03:39] but you can invest in Google, and you can invest in other companies in space,
[03:39 - 03:41] and other companies across a variety of industries.
[03:41 - 03:46] So the company-specific risks tend to cancel themselves out, and therefore,
[03:46 - 03:50] you don't get compensated via a higher return for taking on those risks.
[03:50 - 03:54] In other words, if you're only invested in Apple, then you're taking on those risks,
[03:54 - 03:57] but you're not going to get compensated for those risks.
[03:57 - 04:04] The next risk is systematic risk, and that is the primary risk that we need to analyze,
[04:04 - 04:11] because that represents the company's sensitivity to something that we can't diversify away from,
[04:11 - 04:12] and that is market risk.
[04:12 - 04:19] We cannot diversify away from market risk, and so investors will demand returns for assuming this risk.
[04:22 - 04:29] And so the framework that the CAPM provides for quantifying that market risk
[04:30 - 04:32] ultimately crystallizes in a formula.
[04:32 - 04:37] This is that cost of equity formula that says that the cost of equity equals the risk-free rate,
[04:37 - 04:41] plus beta times an equity risk premium.
[04:42 - 04:48] The beta represents systematic risk of the company's sensitivity,
[04:48 - 04:55] or in other words, the company's sensitivity that you're looking at to the overall market.
[04:56 - 05:04] So the more sensitive the company that you're looking at is to the overall market,
[05:04 - 05:06] the higher the beta will be.
[05:07 - 05:11] In other words, every time the market goes up by 1%, and we expect this company,
[05:11 - 05:13] because it's highly sensitive to market fluctuations,
[05:13 - 05:17] it would go up by 3%, that would have a beta of 3.
[05:17 - 05:21] And conversely, if the market declines, if the overall market declines,
[05:21 - 05:23] and you have a very high sensitivity to that,
[05:23 - 05:28] so if the market declines by 1%, and because you are extremely sensitive to market fluctuations,
[05:28 - 05:32] you are business declines by 3%, you have a beta of 3, that's what the beta represents
[05:32 - 05:34] the sensitivity to market risk.
[05:35 - 05:41] The equity risk premium reflects the risk of just investing in equities as a class.
[05:41 - 05:45] So by virtue of just being invested in equities, you're taking on equity risk,
[05:46 - 05:49] and that's the premium above the risk-free rate that you would expect in investor
[05:49 - 05:51] and in equities to expect.

[00:01 - 00:11] So, the risk free rate should theoretically reflect the yield to maturity of a default free government bond of equivalent maturity to the duration of the cash flows that are being discounted.
[00:11 - 00:24] And so, since we don't have something that exactly provides the equivalent maturity because we are building a DCF for a company where we're stretching a cash flows effectively in perpetuity,
[00:24 - 00:33] the most common proxy that we use to calculate the risk free rate is the 10-year US bond.
[00:33 - 00:41] That's the preferred proxy for the risk free rate in the US and similar 10-year securities exist in Germany and Japan.
[00:41 - 00:43] Those are considered effectively risk free in those markets.
[00:43 - 00:54] So, if you're looking at European companies or Asian companies, the 10-year German bond and the 10-year Japanese bond are the appropriate risk free rates in those markets.
[00:54 - 01:07] And we see sort of the yield as of the printing here or as of the recording of this video as 2.5% for the 10-year US, 1.1% for the German, and 0.5% for the Japanese 10-year.
[01:07 - 01:13] The equity risk premium represents how much extra return investor seek for investing in equities as a class.
[01:13 - 01:21] The prevalent approach compares historical spreads between the S&P 500 and the yield on 10-year treasuries.
[01:21 - 01:46] And so, it looks at spreads typically over 80 or the last 80 years and says, okay, on average, the return of being invested just in the S&P 500 over the 10-year risk free bonds has been 5% to 8% and that's generally the accepted sort of range depending on the methodology of how you go about calculating those averages over the last 80 years or maybe you pick shorter time frames.
[01:46 - 01:50] And those are several competing methodologies for how to do that.
[01:50 - 01:59] But the general accepted range in practice or the bottom line is you're looking at 5% to 8% is an acceptable equity risk premium for public companies.
[01:59 - 02:02] Ibitsin, which is part of Morningstar, is a leading source for this.
[02:02 - 02:14] And so, when you're trying to understand sort of what is the equity risk premium, those are usually defined and quantified by Ibitsin as the leading sort of stuff and felt is another one, but if it's in the certainly the leading one.
[02:14 - 02:26] It should be noted that even though most of the sort of, most of the debate centers around beta and the problems surrounding beta, the equity risk premium is also highly debated.
[02:26 - 02:42] The fact that you use historical, the historical excess premiums of the S&P 500 over 10-year US treasuries, the fact that practitioners use that predominantly to define what they think the expected future equity risk premium is going to be, is also highly contested.
[02:42 - 02:55] Obviously, there's obviously the implication implicitly here that what has happened in terms of the returns over the last 80 years is what investors now expect when they put capital to work.
[02:55 - 03:05] There are some debates about whether that is the most appropriate way to do it, but that is in fact the prevailing way in which practitioners try to quantify the equity risk premium.
[03:05 - 03:13] Some nuances in terms of equity risk premium for those that are modeling, for those of you that are going to be modeling small companies, certainly Apple doesn't have to deal with this.
[03:13 - 03:25] But those of you that are dealing with companies below a $4 billion market cap, we've included some general benchmarks for what are premiums in excess of the general equity risk premium that you should be incorporating.
[03:25 - 03:33] So in practice, an additional premium is added to the equity risk premium when analyzing small companies and companies operating in higher risk countries.
[03:33 - 03:44] So first the small cap premiums are listed here and understand that the calculation for this premium is typically added separately from the ERP.
[03:44 - 03:48] In other words, the beta doesn't affect the small cap premium, there's some debate about whether it should.
[03:48 - 04:00] And ultimately boils down to do we think that the company that the company that we're analyzing has an added sensitivity to the small cap premium itself sensitive to that small cap premium.
[04:00 - 04:10] Ultimately most practitioners will exclude the small cap premium from the, you know, will not allow beta to factor into the small cap premium.
[04:10 - 04:13] And so this is what the formula will end up looking like.
[04:13 - 04:16] And then for country risk, it's the same issue.
[04:16 - 04:19] An additional premium is added when you're looking at countries.
[04:19 - 04:25] So countries that have a higher risk.
[04:25 - 04:37] So if you're investing in a company that operates in a higher risk environment, it is usually the case that you're going to add some sort of premium to reflect that higher risk.
[04:37 - 04:42] That the country where the business operates and imposes.
[04:42 - 04:49] And so here we've identified some of the biggest economies and what their excess country risk premiums are going to look like.
[04:49 - 04:55] As you would expect, most developed countries have a marginal if any sort of excess country risk premium.
[04:55 - 05:01] But then other more developing countries and regions have higher country risk premiums.
[05:01 - 05:06] And so we've included in your notes a full breakdown by country, but these are some of the bigies.

[00:00 - 00:07] Now let's talk about interpreting beta so a company whose equity has a beta of one has on average
[00:07 - 00:13] Scene returns in line with the overall stock market. That's what a beta of one represents if historically
[00:14 - 00:19] The returns have been the same you would expect the returns to continue to be the same with the market

[00:27 - 00:33] Company with a beta of two has on average Scene returns rise twice as faster drop twice as fast as the overall market
